<p>Titanium is the ninth-most <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_elements_in_Earth%27s_crust" title="Abundance of elements in Earth's crust">abundant</a> element in <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s crust (0.63% by <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barksdale1968p732_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barksdale1968p732-20">[20]</a></sup> and the seventh-most abundant metal. It is present as oxides in most <a href="/wiki/Igneous_rock" title="Igneous rock">igneous rocks</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">sediments</a> derived from them, in living things, and natural bodies of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBC_5-6"><a href="#cite_note-EBC-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-6">[6]</a></sup> Of the 801 types of igneous rocks analyzed by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey" title="United States Geological Survey">United States Geological Survey</a>, 784 contained titanium. Its proportion in soils is approximately 0.5 to 1.5%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barksdale1968p732_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Barksdale1968p732-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Common titanium-containing <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">minerals</a> are <a href="/wiki/Anatase" title="Anatase">anatase</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brookite" title="Brookite">brookite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ilmenite" title="Ilmenite">ilmenite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Perovskite" title="Perovskite">perovskite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rutile" title="Rutile">rutile</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Titanite" title="Titanite">titanite</a> (sphene).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p453_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p453-16">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Akaogiite" title="Akaogiite">Akaogiite</a> is an extremely rare mineral consisting of titanium dioxide. Of these minerals, only rutile and ilmenite have economic importance, yet even they are difficult to find in high concentrations. About 6.0 and 0.7 million tonnes of those minerals were mined in 2011, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-19">[19]</a></sup> Significant titanium-bearing ilmenite deposits exist in western <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone" title="Sierra Leone">Sierra Leone</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p453_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p453-16">[16]</a></sup> About 186,000 tonnes of titanium <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metal_sponge" title="Metal sponge">metal sponge</a> were produced in 2011, mostly in China (60,000 t), Japan (56,000 t), Russia (40,000 t), United States (32,000 t) and Kazakhstan (20,700 t). Total reserves of titanium are estimated to exceed 600 million tonnes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>The concentration of titanium is about 4 picomolar in the ocean. At 100 °C, the concentration of titanium in water is estimated to be less than 10<sup>−7</sup> M at pH 7. The identity of titanium species in aqueous solution remains unknown because of its low solubility and the lack of sensitive spectroscopic methods, although only the 4+ oxidation state is stable in air. No evidence exists for a biological role, although rare organisms are known to accumulate high concentrations of titanium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Titanium is contained in <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a>, and it has been detected in the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Stellar_classification" title="Stellar classification">M-type</a> <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-6">[6]</a></sup> (the coolest type) with a surface temperature of 3,200 °C (5,790 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p451_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p451-22">[22]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">Rocks</a> brought back from the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_17" title="Apollo 17">Apollo 17</a> mission are composed of 12.1% TiO<sub>2</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-6">[6]</a></sup> It is also found in <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a> ash, <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a>, and even the <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">human</a> body. Native titanium (pure metallic) is very rare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_titanium" title="Isotopes of titanium">Isotopes of titanium</a></div>
<p>Naturally occurring titanium is composed of 5 stable <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>: <sup>46</sup>Ti, <sup>47</sup>Ti, <sup>48</sup>Ti, <sup>49</sup>Ti, and <sup>50</sup>Ti, with <sup>48</sup>Ti being the most abundant (73.8% <a href="/wiki/Natural_abundance" title="Natural abundance">natural abundance</a>). Eleven <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioisotope" title="Radioisotope">radioisotopes</a> have been characterized, the most stable being <sup>44</sup>Ti with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 63 years; <sup>45</sup>Ti, 184.8 minutes; <sup>51</sup>Ti, 5.76 minutes; and <sup>52</sup>Ti, 1.7 minutes. All the other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactive" title="Radioactive">radioactive</a> isotopes have half-lives less than 33 seconds and the majority, less than half a second.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EnvChem_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-EnvChem-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>The isotopes of titanium range in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_weight" title="Atomic weight">atomic weight</a> from 39.99 <a href="/wiki/Unified_atomic_mass_unit" title="Unified atomic mass unit">u</a> (<sup>40</sup>Ti) to 57.966 u (<sup>58</sup>Ti). The primary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decay_mode" title="Decay mode">decay mode</a> before the most abundant stable isotope, <sup>48</sup>Ti, is <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a> and the primary mode after is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beta_emission" title="Beta emission">beta emission</a>. The primary <a href="/wiki/Decay_product" title="Decay product">decay products</a> before <sup>48</sup>Ti are element 21 (<a href="/wiki/Scandium" title="Scandium">scandium</a>) isotopes and the primary products after are element 23 (<a href="/wiki/Vanadium" title="Vanadium">vanadium</a>) isotopes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EnvChem_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-EnvChem-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Titanium becomes radioactive upon bombardment with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deuterons" title="Deuterons">deuterons</a>, emitting mainly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Positrons" title="Positrons">positrons</a> and hard <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamma_rays" title="Gamma rays">gamma rays</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_6-6"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">Compounds</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref" role="note">See also: the categories <a href="/wiki/Category:Titanium_compounds" title="Category:Titanium compounds">Titanium compounds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Category:Titanium_minerals" title="Category:Titanium minerals">Titanium minerals</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:62px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titanium_nitride_coating.jpg"><img alt="A steel colored twist drill bit with the spiral groove colored in a golden shade." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1016" data-file-width="165" height="369" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Titanium_nitride_coating.jpg/60px-Titanium_nitride_coating.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Titanium_nitride_coating.jpg/90px-Titanium_nitride_coating.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Titanium_nitride_coating.jpg/120px-Titanium_nitride_coating.jpg 2x" width="60"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titanium_nitride_coating.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>TiN-coated <a href="/wiki/Drill" title="Drill">drill</a> bit</div></div></div>
<p>The +4 <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation state</a> dominates titanium chemistry,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1997p958_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1997p958-24">[24]</a></sup> but compounds in the +3 <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation state</a> are also common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1997p970_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1997p970-25">[25]</a></sup> Commonly, titanium adopts an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Octahedral_coordination_geometry" title="Octahedral coordination geometry">octahedral coordination geometry</a> in its complexes, but tetrahedral TiCl<sub>4</sub> is a notable exception. Because of its high oxidation state, titanium(IV) compounds exhibit a high degree of <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent bonding</a>. Unlike most other transition metals, simple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transition_metal_aquo_complex" title="Transition metal aquo complex">aquo Ti(IV) complexes</a> are unknown.
</p>
<h3><span id="Oxides.2C_sulfides.2C_and_alkoxides"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxides,_sulfides,_and_alkoxides">Oxides, sulfides, and alkoxides</span></h3>
<p>The most important oxide is TiO<sub>2</sub>, which exists in three important <a href="/wiki/Polymorphism_(materials_science)" title="Polymorphism (materials science)">polymorphs</a>; <a href="/wiki/Anatase" title="Anatase">anatase</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brookite" title="Brookite">brookite</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rutile" title="Rutile">rutile</a>. All of these are white diamagnetic solids, although mineral samples can appear dark (see <a href="/wiki/Rutile" title="Rutile">rutile</a>). They adopt polymeric structures in which Ti is surrounded by six <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">oxide</a> ligands that link to other Ti centers.
</p><p>The term <i><a href="/wiki/Titanate" title="Titanate">titanates</a></i> usually refers to titanium(IV) compounds, as represented by <a href="/wiki/Barium_titanate" title="Barium titanate">barium titanate</a> (BaTiO<sub>3</sub>). With a <a href="/wiki/Perovskite" title="Perovskite">perovskite</a> structure, this material exhibits <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Piezoelectric" title="Piezoelectric">piezoelectric</a> properties and is used as a transducer in the interconversion of <a href="/wiki/Sound" title="Sound">sound</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TICE6th_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-TICE6th-10">[10]</a></sup> Many minerals are titanates, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Ilmenite" title="Ilmenite">ilmenite</a> (FeTiO<sub>3</sub>). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Star_sapphire_(jewel)" title="Star sapphire (jewel)">Star sapphires</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ruby" title="Ruby">rubies</a> get their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asterism_(gemmology)" title="Asterism (gemmology)">asterism</a> (star-forming shine) from the presence of titanium dioxide impurities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p453_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p453-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>A variety of reduced oxides of titanium are known. Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, described as a Ti(IV)-Ti(III) species, is a purple semiconductor produced by reduction of TiO<sub>2</sub> with hydrogen at high temperatures,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> and is used industrially when surfaces need to be vapour-coated with titanium dioxide: it evaporates as pure TiO, whereas TiO<sub>2</sub> evaporates as a mixture of oxides and deposits coatings with variable refractive index.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Also known is <a href="/wiki/Titanium(III)_oxide" title="Titanium(III) oxide">Ti<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub></a>, with the <a href="/wiki/Corundum" title="Corundum">corundum</a> structure, and <a href="/wiki/Titanium(II)_oxide" title="Titanium(II) oxide">TiO</a>, with the rock salt structure, although often nonstoichiometric.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Alkoxide" title="Alkoxide">alkoxides</a> of titanium(IV), prepared by reacting TiCl<sub>4</sub> with alcohols, are colourless compounds that convert to the dioxide on reaction with water. They are industrially useful for depositing solid TiO<sub>2</sub> via the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sol-gel_process" title="Sol-gel process">sol-gel process</a>. <a href="/wiki/Titanium_isopropoxide" title="Titanium isopropoxide">Titanium isopropoxide</a> is used in the synthesis of chiral organic compounds via the <a href="/wiki/Sharpless_epoxidation" title="Sharpless epoxidation">Sharpless epoxidation</a>.
</p><p>Titanium forms a variety of sulfides, but only <a href="/wiki/Titanium_disulfide" title="Titanium disulfide">TiS<sub>2</sub></a> has attracted significant interest. It adopts a layered structure and was used as a cathode in the development of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lithium_batteries" title="Lithium batteries">lithium batteries</a>. Because Ti(IV) is a <a href="/wiki/HSAB_theory" title="HSAB theory">"hard cation"</a>, the sulfides of titanium are unstable and tend to hydrolyze to the oxide with release of hydrogen sulfide.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nitrides_and_carbides">Nitrides and carbides</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Titanium_nitride" title="Titanium nitride">Titanium nitride</a> (TiN) is a member of a family of refractory transition metal nitrides and exhibits properties similar to both covalent compounds including; thermodynamic stability, extreme hardness, thermal/electrical conductivity, and a high melting point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> TiN has a hardness equivalent to <a href="/wiki/Sapphire" title="Sapphire">sapphire</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carborundum" title="Carborundum">carborundum</a> (9.0 on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohs_Scale" title="Mohs Scale">Mohs Scale</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> and is often used to coat cutting tools, such as <a href="/wiki/Drill_bit" title="Drill bit">drill bits</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> It is also used as a gold-colored decorative finish and as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copper-based_chips#Barrier_metal" title="Copper-based chips">barrier metal</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semiconductor_fabrication" title="Semiconductor fabrication">semiconductor fabrication</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Titanium_carbide" title="Titanium carbide">Titanium carbide</a>, which is also very hard, is found in cutting tools and coatings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TiCl3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="666" data-file-width="458" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/TiCl3.jpg/100px-TiCl3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/TiCl3.jpg/150px-TiCl3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/TiCl3.jpg/200px-TiCl3.jpg 2x" width="100"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TiCl3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Titanium(III) compounds are characteristically violet, illustrated by this aqueous solution of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titanium_trichloride" title="Titanium trichloride">titanium trichloride</a>.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Halides">Halides</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Titanium_tetrachloride" title="Titanium tetrachloride">Titanium tetrachloride</a> (titanium(IV) chloride, TiCl<sub>4</sub><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>) is a colorless volatile liquid (commercial samples are yellowish) that, in air, hydrolyzes with spectacular emission of white clouds. Via the <a href="/wiki/Kroll_process" title="Kroll process">Kroll process</a>, TiCl<sub>4</sub> is produced in the conversion of titanium ores to titanium dioxide, e.g., for use in white paint.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> It is widely used in <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic chemistry</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Lewis_acids_and_bases" title="Lewis acids and bases">Lewis acid</a>, for example in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mukaiyama_aldol_condensation" title="Mukaiyama aldol condensation">Mukaiyama aldol condensation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Van_Arkel_process" title="Van Arkel process">van Arkel process</a>, <a href="/wiki/Titanium_tetraiodide" title="Titanium tetraiodide">titanium tetraiodide</a> (TiI<sub>4</sub>) is generated in the production of high purity titanium metal.
</p><p>Titanium(III) and titanium(II) also form stable chlorides. A notable example is <a href="/wiki/Titanium(III)_chloride" title="Titanium(III) chloride">titanium(III) chloride</a> (TiCl<sub>3</sub>), which is used as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a> for production of <a href="/wiki/Polyolefin" title="Polyolefin">polyolefins</a> (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ziegler-Natta_catalyst" title="Ziegler-Natta catalyst">Ziegler-Natta catalyst</a>) and a reducing <a href="/wiki/Reagent" title="Reagent">agent</a> in organic chemistry.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organometallic_complexes">Organometallic complexes</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organotitanium_chemistry" title="Organotitanium chemistry">Organotitanium chemistry</a></div>
<p>Owing to the important role of titanium compounds as <a href="/wiki/Polymerization" title="Polymerization">polymerization</a> catalyst, compounds with Ti-C bonds have been intensively studied. The most common organotitanium complex is <a href="/wiki/Titanocene_dichloride" title="Titanocene dichloride">titanocene dichloride</a> ((C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>5</sub>)<sub>2</sub>TiCl<sub>2</sub>). Related compounds include <a href="/wiki/Tebbe%27s_reagent" title="Tebbe's reagent">Tebbe's reagent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Petasis_reagent" title="Petasis reagent">Petasis reagent</a>. Titanium forms <a href="/wiki/Metal_carbonyl" title="Metal carbonyl">carbonyl complexes</a>, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Titanocene_dicarbonyl" title="Titanocene dicarbonyl">(C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>5</sub>)<sub>2</sub>Ti(CO)<sub>2</sub></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anticancer_therapy">Anticancer therapy</span></h3>
<p>Following the success of  <a href="/wiki/Cisplatin" title="Cisplatin">platinum-based</a> chemotherapy, titanium(IV) complexes were among the first non-platinum compounds to be tested for cancer treatment. The advantage of titanium compounds lies in their high efficacy and low toxicity. In biological environments, hydrolysis leads to the safe and inert titanium dioxide. Despite these advantages the first candidate compounds failed clinical trials. Further development resulted in the creation of potentially effective, selective, and stable titanium-based drugs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Their mode of action is not yet well understood.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Martin_Heinrich_Klaproth.jpg"><img alt="Engraved profile image of a mid-age male with high forehead. The person is wearing a coat and a neckerchief." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1108" data-file-width="848" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Martin_Heinrich_Klaproth.jpg/170px-Martin_Heinrich_Klaproth.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Martin_Heinrich_Klaproth.jpg/255px-Martin_Heinrich_Klaproth.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Martin_Heinrich_Klaproth.jpg/340px-Martin_Heinrich_Klaproth.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Martin_Heinrich_Klaproth.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Martin_Heinrich_Klaproth" title="Martin Heinrich Klaproth">Martin Heinrich Klaproth</a> named titanium for the <a href="/wiki/Titan_(mythology)" title="Titan (mythology)">Titans</a> of <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a></div></div></div>
<p>Titanium was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Discovery_of_the_chemical_elements" title="Discovery of the chemical elements">discovered</a> in 1791 by the <a href="/wiki/Clergy" title="Clergy">clergyman</a> and amateur geologist, <a href="/wiki/William_Gregor" title="William Gregor">William Gregor</a>, as an <a href="/wiki/Inclusion_(mineral)" title="Inclusion (mineral)">inclusion</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">mineral</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a>, Great Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p452_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p452-39">[39]</a></sup> Gregor recognized the presence of a new element in <a href="/wiki/Ilmenite" title="Ilmenite">ilmenite</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HistoryAndUse_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-HistoryAndUse-7">[7]</a></sup> when he found black sand by a stream and noticed the sand was attracted by a <a href="/wiki/Magnet" title="Magnet">magnet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p452_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p452-39">[39]</a></sup> Analyzing the sand, he determined the presence of two metal oxides: <a href="/wiki/Iron_oxide" title="Iron oxide">iron oxide</a> (explaining the attraction to the magnet) and 45.25% of a white metallic oxide he could not identify.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barksdale1968p732_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-Barksdale1968p732-20">[20]</a></sup> Realizing that the unidentified oxide contained a metal that did not match any known element, Gregor reported his findings to the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Geological_Society_of_Cornwall" title="Royal Geological Society of Cornwall">Royal Geological Society of Cornwall</a> and in the German science journal <i><a href="/wiki/Crell%27s_Annalen" title="Crell's Annalen">Crell's Annalen</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p452_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p452-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Around the same time, <a href="/wiki/Franz-Joseph_M%C3%BCller_von_Reichenstein" title="Franz-Joseph Müller von Reichenstein">Franz-Joseph Müller von Reichenstein</a> produced a similar substance, but could not identify it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HistoryAndUse_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-HistoryAndUse-7">[7]</a></sup> The oxide was independently rediscovered in 1795 by <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussian</a> chemist <a href="/wiki/Martin_Heinrich_Klaproth" title="Martin Heinrich Klaproth">Martin Heinrich Klaproth</a> in rutile from Boinik (German name Bajmócska), a village in Hungary (now Bojničky in Slovakia).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p452_39-3"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p452-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Klaproth found that it contained a new element and named it for the <a href="/wiki/Titan_(mythology)" title="Titan (mythology)">Titans</a> of <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p451_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p451-22">[22]</a></sup> After hearing about Gregor's earlier discovery, he obtained a sample of manaccanite and confirmed that it contained titanium.
</p><p>The currently known processes for extracting titanium from its various ores are laborious and costly; it is not possible to reduce the ore by heating with <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> (as in iron smelting) because titanium combines with the carbon to produce <a href="/wiki/Titanium_carbide" title="Titanium carbide">titanium carbide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p452_39-4"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p452-39">[39]</a></sup> Pure metallic titanium (99.9%) was first prepared in 1910 by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Matthew_A._Hunter" title="Matthew A. Hunter">Matthew A. Hunter</a> at <a href="/wiki/Rensselaer_Polytechnic_Institute" title="Rensselaer Polytechnic Institute">Rensselaer Polytechnic Institute</a> by heating TiCl<sub>4</sub> with <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a> at 700–800 °C under great pressure<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roza2008p9_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roza2008p9-43">[43]</a></sup> in a <a href="/wiki/Batch_production" title="Batch production">batch process</a> known as the <a href="/wiki/Hunter_process" title="Hunter process">Hunter process</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_6-7"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-6">[6]</a></sup> Titanium metal was not used outside the laboratory until 1932 when <a href="/wiki/William_Justin_Kroll" title="William Justin Kroll">William Justin Kroll</a> proved that it can be produced by reducing <a href="/wiki/Titanium_tetrachloride" title="Titanium tetrachloride">titanium tetrachloride</a> (TiCl<sub>4</sub>) with <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1997p955_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1997p955-44">[44]</a></sup> Eight years later he refined this process with <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a> and even sodium in what became known as the <a href="/wiki/Kroll_process" title="Kroll process">Kroll process</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1997p955_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1997p955-44">[44]</a></sup> Although research continues into more efficient and cheaper processes (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FFC_Cambridge" title="FFC Cambridge">FFC Cambridge</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Armstrong_process&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Armstrong process (page does not exist)">Armstrong</a>), the Kroll process is still used for commercial production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_6-8"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HistoryAndUse_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-HistoryAndUse-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TitaniumMetal_jpg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="451" data-file-width="600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/TitaniumMetal_jpg.jpg/220px-TitaniumMetal_jpg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/TitaniumMetal_jpg.jpg/330px-TitaniumMetal_jpg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/TitaniumMetal_jpg.jpg/440px-TitaniumMetal_jpg.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TitaniumMetal_jpg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Titanium sponge, made by the <a href="/wiki/Kroll_process" title="Kroll process">Kroll process</a></div></div></div>
<p>Titanium of very high purity was made in small quantities when <a href="/wiki/Anton_Eduard_van_Arkel" title="Anton Eduard van Arkel">Anton Eduard van Arkel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jan_Hendrik_de_Boer" title="Jan Hendrik de Boer">Jan Hendrik de Boer</a> discovered the iodide, or <a href="/wiki/Crystal_bar_process" title="Crystal bar process">crystal bar</a>, process in 1925, by reacting with iodine and decomposing the formed vapours over a hot filament to pure metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1950s and 1960s, the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> pioneered the use of titanium in military and submarine applications<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roza2008p9_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Roza2008p9-43">[43]</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Alfa-class_submarine" title="Alfa-class submarine">Alfa class</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soviet_submarine_K-278_Komsomolets" title="Soviet submarine K-278 Komsomolets">Mike class</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> as part of programs related to the Cold War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Starting in the early 1950s, titanium came into use extensively in military aviation, particularly in high-performance jets, starting with aircraft such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F-100_Super_Sabre" title="F-100 Super Sabre">F-100 Super Sabre</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_A-12" title="Lockheed A-12">Lockheed A-12</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SR-71" title="SR-71">SR-71</a>.
</p><p>Recognizing the strategic importance of titanium,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> the U.S. <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="United States Department of Defense">Department of Defense</a> supported early efforts of commercialization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Throughout the period of the Cold War, titanium was considered a <a href="/wiki/Strategic_material" title="Strategic material">strategic material</a> by the U.S. government, and a large stockpile of titanium sponge was maintained by the <a href="/wiki/Defense_National_Stockpile_Center" title="Defense National Stockpile Center">Defense National Stockpile Center</a>, which was finally depleted in the 2000s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> According to 2006 data, the world's largest producer, Russian-based <a href="/wiki/VSMPO-AVISMA" title="VSMPO-AVISMA">VSMPO-AVISMA</a>, was estimated to account for about 29% of the world market share.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> As of 2015, titanium sponge metal was produced in six countries: China, Japan, Russia, Kazakhstan, the US, Ukraine, and India. (in order of output).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2006, the U.S. <a href="/wiki/DARPA" title="DARPA">Defense Advanced Research Projects Agency</a> (DARPA) awarded $5.7 million to a two-company consortium to develop a new process for making titanium metal <a href="/wiki/Powder_metallurgy" title="Powder metallurgy">powder</a>. Under heat and pressure, the powder can be used to create strong, lightweight items ranging from armour plating to components for the aerospace, transport, and chemical processing industries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production_and_fabrication">Production and fabrication</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TitaniumUSGOV.jpg"><img alt="A small heap of uniform black grains smaller than 1mm diameter." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="451" data-file-width="498" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/TitaniumUSGOV.jpg/220px-TitaniumUSGOV.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/TitaniumUSGOV.jpg/330px-TitaniumUSGOV.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/TitaniumUSGOV.jpg/440px-TitaniumUSGOV.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TitaniumUSGOV.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Titanium (mineral concentrate)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titanium_products.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1931" data-file-width="2551" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Titanium_products.jpg/220px-Titanium_products.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Titanium_products.jpg/330px-Titanium_products.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Titanium_products.jpg/440px-Titanium_products.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titanium_products.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Basic titanium products: plate, tube, rods, and powder</div></div></div>
<p>The processing of titanium metal occurs in four major steps:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> reduction of titanium ore into "sponge", a porous form; melting of sponge, or sponge plus a master alloy to form an ingot; primary fabrication, where an ingot is converted into general mill products such as <a href="/wiki/Bar_stock" title="Bar stock">billet</a>, bar, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plate_(metal)" title="Plate (metal)">plate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheet_metal" title="Sheet metal">sheet</a>, strip, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tube_(fluid_conveyance)" title="Tube (fluid conveyance)">tube</a>; and secondary fabrication of finished shapes from mill products.
</p>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kroll_process" title="Kroll process">Kroll process</a></div>
<p>Because it cannot be readily produced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reduction_(chemistry)" title="Reduction (chemistry)">reduction</a> of its dioxide,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stwertka1998_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-Stwertka1998-12">[12]</a></sup> titanium metal is obtained by reduction of <a href="/wiki/Titanium_tetrachloride" title="Titanium tetrachloride">TiCl<sub>4</sub></a> with magnesium metal in the Kroll process. The complexity of this batch production in the Kroll process explains the relatively high market value of titanium,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barksdale1968p733_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barksdale1968p733-56">[56]</a></sup> despite the Kroll process being less expensive than the Hunter process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roza2008p9_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-Roza2008p9-43">[43]</a></sup> To produce the TiCl<sub>4</sub> required by the Kroll process, the dioxide is subjected to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbothermic_reduction" title="Carbothermic reduction">carbothermic reduction</a> in the presence of <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a>. In this process, the chlorine gas is passed over a red-hot mixture of rutile or ilmenite in the presence of carbon. After extensive purification by <a href="/wiki/Fractional_distillation" title="Fractional distillation">fractional distillation</a>, the TiCl<sub>4</sub> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reduction_(chemistry)" title="Reduction (chemistry)">reduced</a> with 800 °C molten <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a> in an <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a> atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TICE6th_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-TICE6th-10">[10]</a></sup> Titanium metal can be further purified by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Van_Arkel%E2%80%93de_Boer_process" title="Van Arkel–de Boer process">van Arkel–de Boer process</a>, which involves <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_vapor_transport" title="Chemical vapor transport">thermal decomposition</a> of titanium tetraiodide.
</p>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/FFC_Cambridge_process" title="FFC Cambridge process">FFC Cambridge process</a></div>
<p>A more recently developed batch production method, the FFC Cambridge process,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> consumes titanium dioxide powder (a refined form of rutile) as feedstock and produces titanium metal, either powder or sponge. The process involves fewer steps than the Kroll process and takes less time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roza2008p23_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roza2008p23-58">[58]</a></sup> If mixed oxide powders are used, the product is an <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloy</a>.
</p><p>Common titanium alloys are made by reduction. For example, cuprotitanium (rutile with <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> added is reduced), ferrocarbon titanium (ilmenite reduced with <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke</a> in an electric furnace), and manganotitanium (rutile with manganese or manganese oxides) are reduced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TI_Encarta2005_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-TI_Encarta2005-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 FeTiO<sub>3</sub> + 7 Cl<sub>2</sub> + 6 C → 2 TiCl<sub>4</sub> + 2 FeCl<sub>3</sub> + 6 CO (900 °C)</dd>
<dd>TiCl<sub>4</sub> + 2 Mg → 2 MgCl<sub>2</sub> + Ti (1,100 °C)</dd></dl>
<p>About fifty <a href="/wiki/Titanium_alloy#Grades" title="Titanium alloy">grades</a> of titanium and titanium alloys are designed and currently used, although only a couple of dozen are readily available commercially.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/ASTM_International" title="ASTM International">ASTM International</a> recognizes 31 <a href="/wiki/Titanium_alloy" title="Titanium alloy">grades of titanium</a> metal and alloys, of which grades one through four are commercially pure (unalloyed). Those four vary in tensile strength as a function of <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> content, with grade 1 being the most ductile (lowest tensile strength with an oxygen content of 0.18%), and grade 4 the least ductile (highest tensile strength with an oxygen content of 0.40%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p453_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p453-16">[16]</a></sup> The remaining grades are alloys, each designed for specific properties of ductility, strength, hardness, electrical resistivity, <a href="/wiki/Creep_(deformation)" title="Creep (deformation)">creep</a> resistance, specific corrosion resistance, and combinations thereof.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to the ASTM specifications, titanium alloys are also produced to meet aerospace and military specifications (SAE-AMS, MIL-T), ISO standards, and country-specific specifications, as well as proprietary end-user specifications for aerospace, military, medical, and industrial applications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Titanium powder is manufactured using a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flow_production" title="Flow production">flow production</a> process known as the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Armstrong_process&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Armstrong process (page does not exist)">Armstrong process</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roza2008p25_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roza2008p25-63">[63]</a></sup> that is similar to the batch production Hunter process. A stream of titanium tetrachloride gas is added to a stream of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molten" title="Molten">molten</a> sodium metal; the products (sodium chloride <a href="/wiki/Salt" title="Salt">salt</a> and titanium particles) is filtered from the extra sodium. Titanium is then separated from the salt by water washing. Both sodium and chlorine are recycled to produce and process more titanium tetrachloride.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECI_online_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-ECI_online-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>All <a href="/wiki/Welding" title="Welding">welding</a> of titanium must be done in an inert atmosphere of <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a> or <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> to shield it from contamination with atmospheric gases (oxygen, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barksdale1968p734_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-Barksdale1968p734-14">[14]</a></sup> Contamination causes a variety of conditions, such as <a href="/wiki/Embrittlement" title="Embrittlement">embrittlement</a>, which reduce the integrity of the assembly welds and lead to joint failure.
</p><p>Commercially pure flat product (sheet, plate) can be formed readily, but processing must take into account the fact that the metal has a "memory" and tends to spring back. This is especially true of certain high-strength alloys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Titanium cannot be <a href="/wiki/Solder" title="Solder">soldered</a> without first pre-<a href="/wiki/Plating" title="Plating">plating</a> it in a metal that is <a href="/wiki/Solderability" title="Solderability">solderable</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The metal can be machined with the same equipment and the same processes as <a href="/wiki/Stainless_steel" title="Stainless steel">stainless steel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barksdale1968p734_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-Barksdale1968p734-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titanzylinder.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1440" data-file-width="2160" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Titanzylinder.jpg/220px-Titanzylinder.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Titanzylinder.jpg/330px-Titanzylinder.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Titanzylinder.jpg/440px-Titanzylinder.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titanzylinder.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A titanium cylinder of "grade 2" quality</div></div></div>
<p>Titanium is used in <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> as an alloying element (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferro-titanium" title="Ferro-titanium">ferro-titanium</a>) to reduce <a href="/wiki/Crystallite" title="Crystallite">grain size</a> and as a deoxidizer, and in stainless steel to reduce carbon content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBC_5-7"><a href="#cite_note-EBC-5">[5]</a></sup> Titanium is often alloyed with aluminium (to refine grain size), <a href="/wiki/Vanadium" title="Vanadium">vanadium</a>, copper (to harden), <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a>, and other metals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ECE738_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-ECE738-68">[68]</a></sup> Titanium mill products (sheet, plate, bar, wire, forgings, castings) find application in industrial, aerospace, recreational, and emerging markets. Powdered titanium is used in <a href="/wiki/Pyrotechnics" title="Pyrotechnics">pyrotechnics</a> as a source of bright-burning particles.
</p>
<h3><span id="Pigments.2C_additives.2C_and_coatings"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Pigments,_additives,_and_coatings">Pigments, additives, and coatings</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titanium(IV)_oxide.jpg"><img alt="Watch glass on a black surface with a small portion of white powder" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="531" data-file-width="727" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Titanium%28IV%29_oxide.jpg/220px-Titanium%28IV%29_oxide.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Titanium%28IV%29_oxide.jpg/330px-Titanium%28IV%29_oxide.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Titanium%28IV%29_oxide.jpg/440px-Titanium%28IV%29_oxide.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titanium(IV)_oxide.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Titanium_dioxide" title="Titanium dioxide">Titanium dioxide</a> is the most commonly used compound of titanium</div></div></div>
<p>About 95% of all titanium ore is destined for refinement into <a href="/wiki/Titanium_dioxide" title="Titanium dioxide">titanium dioxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">Ti<a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">O</a><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>), an intensely white permanent <a href="/wiki/Pigment" title="Pigment">pigment</a> used in paints, paper, toothpaste, and plastics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-19">[19]</a></sup> It is also used in cement, in gemstones, as an optical opacifier in paper,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> and a strengthening agent in graphite composite fishing rods and golf clubs.
</p><p><span class="chemf nowrap">TiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> powder is chemically inert, resists fading in sunlight, and is very opaque: it imparts a pure and brilliant white colour to the brown or grey chemicals that form the majority of household plastics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HistoryAndUse_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-HistoryAndUse-7">[7]</a></sup> In nature, this compound is found in the minerals <a href="/wiki/Anatase" title="Anatase">anatase</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brookite" title="Brookite">brookite</a>, and rutile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBC_5-8"><a href="#cite_note-EBC-5">[5]</a></sup> Paint made with titanium dioxide does well in severe temperatures and marine environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HistoryAndUse_7-6"><a href="#cite_note-HistoryAndUse-7">[7]</a></sup> Pure titanium dioxide has a very high <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">index of refraction</a> and an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_dispersion" title="Optical dispersion">optical dispersion</a> higher than <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamond</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_6-9"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-6">[6]</a></sup> In addition to being a very important pigment, titanium dioxide is also used in sunscreens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stwertka1998_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-Stwertka1998-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aerospace_and_marine">Aerospace and marine</span></h3>
<p>Because titanium alloys have high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tensile_strength" title="Tensile strength">tensile strength</a> to density ratio,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TICE6th_10-5"><a href="#cite_note-TICE6th-10">[10]</a></sup> high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corrosion_resistance" title="Corrosion resistance">corrosion resistance</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_6-10"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-6">[6]</a></sup> fatigue resistance, high crack resistance,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moiseyev_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moiseyev-70">[70]</a></sup> and ability to withstand moderately high temperatures without <a href="/wiki/Creep_(deformation)" title="Creep (deformation)">creeping</a>, they are used in aircraft, armour plating, naval ships, spacecraft, and missiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_6-11"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HistoryAndUse_7-7"><a href="#cite_note-HistoryAndUse-7">[7]</a></sup> For these applications, <a href="/wiki/Titanium_alloy" title="Titanium alloy">titanium is alloyed</a> with aluminium, zirconium, nickel,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT7513_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT7513-71">[71]</a></sup> vanadium, and other elements to manufacture a variety of components including critical structural parts, fire walls, <a href="/wiki/Landing_gear" title="Landing gear">landing gear</a>, exhaust ducts (helicopters), and hydraulic systems. In fact, about two thirds of all titanium metal produced is used in aircraft engines and frames.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p454_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p454-72">[72]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titanium_6AL-4V" title="Titanium 6AL-4V">titanium 6AL-4V</a> alloy accounts for almost 50% of all alloys used in aircraft applications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_A-12" title="Lockheed A-12">Lockheed A-12</a> and its development the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SR-71_Blackbird" title="SR-71 Blackbird">SR-71 "Blackbird"</a> were two of the first aircraft frames where titanium was used, paving the way for much wider use in modern military and commercial aircraft. An estimated 59 metric tons (130,000 pounds) are used in the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_777" title="Boeing 777">Boeing 777</a>, 45 in the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_747" title="Boeing 747">Boeing 747</a>, 18 in the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737" title="Boeing 737">Boeing 737</a>, 32 in the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A340" title="Airbus A340">Airbus A340</a>, 18 in the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A330" title="Airbus A330">Airbus A330</a>, and 12 in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Airbus_A320" title="Airbus A320">Airbus A320</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A380" title="Airbus A380">Airbus A380</a> may use 77 metric tons, including about 11 tons in the engines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> In aero engine applications, titanium is used for rotors, compressor blades, hydraulic system components, and <a href="/wiki/Nacelle" title="Nacelle">nacelles</a>. An early use in jet engines was for the <a href="/wiki/Orenda_Iroquois" title="Orenda Iroquois">Orenda Iroquois</a> in the 1950s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>412</span></sup>
</p><p>Because titanium is resistant to corrosion by sea water, it is used to make propeller shafts, rigging, and <a href="/wiki/Heat_exchanger" title="Heat exchanger">heat exchangers</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Desalination_plant" title="Desalination plant">desalination plants</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_6-12"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-6">[6]</a></sup> heater-chillers for salt water aquariums, fishing line and leader, and divers' knives. Titanium is used in the housings and components of ocean-deployed surveillance and monitoring devices for science and the military. The former <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> developed techniques for making submarines with hulls of titanium alloys<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> forging titanium in huge vacuum tubes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT7513_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT7513-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Titanium is used in the walls of the Juno spacecraft's <a href="/wiki/Juno_Radiation_Vault" title="Juno Radiation Vault">vault</a> to shield on-board electronics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial">Industrial</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hochreines_Titan_(99.999)_mit_sichtbarer_Kristallstruktur.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Hochreines_Titan_%2899.999%29_mit_sichtbarer_Kristallstruktur.jpg/220px-Hochreines_Titan_%2899.999%29_mit_sichtbarer_Kristallstruktur.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Hochreines_Titan_%2899.999%29_mit_sichtbarer_Kristallstruktur.jpg/330px-Hochreines_Titan_%2899.999%29_mit_sichtbarer_Kristallstruktur.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Hochreines_Titan_%2899.999%29_mit_sichtbarer_Kristallstruktur.jpg/440px-Hochreines_Titan_%2899.999%29_mit_sichtbarer_Kristallstruktur.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hochreines_Titan_(99.999)_mit_sichtbarer_Kristallstruktur.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>High-purity (99.999%) titanium with visible <a href="/wiki/Crystallite" title="Crystallite">crystallites</a></div></div></div>
<p>Welded titanium pipe and process equipment (heat exchangers, tanks, process vessels, valves) are used in the chemical and petrochemical industries primarily for corrosion resistance. Specific alloys are used in oil and gas downhole applications and <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a> <a href="/wiki/Hydrometallurgy" title="Hydrometallurgy">hydrometallurgy</a> for their high strength (e. g.: titanium beta C alloy), corrosion resistance, or both. The <a href="/wiki/Pulp_and_paper_industry" title="Pulp and paper industry">pulp and paper industry</a> uses titanium in process equipment exposed to corrosive media, such as sodium hypochlorite or wet chlorine gas (in the bleachery).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Other applications include <a href="/wiki/Ultrasonic_welding" title="Ultrasonic welding">ultrasonic welding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wave_soldering" title="Wave soldering">wave soldering</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sputtering" title="Sputtering">sputtering</a> targets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Titanium_tetrachloride" title="Titanium tetrachloride">Titanium tetrachloride</a> (TiCl<sub>4</sub>), a colorless liquid, is important as an intermediate in the process of making TiO<sub>2</sub> and is also used to produce the <a href="/wiki/Ziegler%E2%80%93Natta_catalyst" title="Ziegler–Natta catalyst">Ziegler–Natta catalyst</a>. Titanium tetrachloride is also used to iridize <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glass</a> and, because it fumes strongly in moist air, it is used to make smoke screens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stwertka1998_12-4"><a href="#cite_note-Stwertka1998-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consumer_and_architectural">Consumer and architectural</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titanium-stamps.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3024" data-file-width="4032" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Titanium-stamps.jpg/220px-Titanium-stamps.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Titanium-stamps.jpg/330px-Titanium-stamps.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Titanium-stamps.jpg/440px-Titanium-stamps.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titanium-stamps.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Titanium <a href="/wiki/Seal_(East_Asia)" title="Seal (East Asia)">sealing stamps</a></div></div></div>
<p>Titanium metal is used in automotive applications, particularly in automobile and motorcycle racing where low weight and high strength and rigidity are critical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> The metal is generally too expensive for the general consumer market, though some late model <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Corvette" title="Chevrolet Corvette">Corvettes</a> have been manufactured with titanium exhausts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GM_small-block_engine" title="GM small-block engine">Corvette Z06's LT4</a> supercharged engine uses lightweight, solid titanium intake valves for greater strength and resistance to heat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>Titanium is used in many sporting goods: tennis rackets, golf clubs, lacrosse stick shafts; cricket, hockey, lacrosse, and football helmet grills, and bicycle frames and components. Although not a mainstream material for bicycle production, titanium bikes have been used by racing teams and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adventure_Cycling" title="Adventure Cycling">adventure cyclists</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>Titanium alloys are used in spectacle frames that are rather expensive but highly durable, long lasting, light weight, and cause no skin allergies. Many backpackers use titanium equipment, including cookware, eating utensils, lanterns, and tent stakes. Though slightly more expensive than traditional steel or aluminium alternatives, titanium products can be significantly lighter without compromising strength. Titanium horseshoes are preferred to steel by <a href="/wiki/Farrier" title="Farrier">farriers</a> because they are lighter and more durable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donachie2000_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Donachie2000-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>Titanium has occasionally been used in architecture. The 42.5-m (139 foot) <a href="/wiki/Monument_to_Yuri_Gagarin" title="Monument to Yuri Gagarin">Monument to Yuri Gagarin</a>, the first man to travel in space (<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Titanium&amp;params=55_42_29.7_N_37_34_57.2_E_region:CN-62_type:landmark"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">55°42′29.7″N</span> <span class="longitude">37°34′57.2″E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">55.708250°N 37.582556°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">55.708250; 37.582556</span></span></span></a></span>), as well as the 110-m (360.9 feet) <a href="/wiki/Monument_to_the_Conquerors_of_Space" title="Monument to the Conquerors of Space">Monument to the Conquerors of Space</a> on top of the <a href="/wiki/Memorial_Museum_of_Cosmonautics" title="Memorial Museum of Cosmonautics">Cosmonaut Museum</a> in Moscow are made of titanium for the metal's attractive colour and association with rocketry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Guggenheim_Museum_Bilbao" title="Guggenheim Museum Bilbao">Guggenheim Museum Bilbao</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cerritos_Millennium_Library" title="Cerritos Millennium Library">Cerritos Millennium Library</a> were the first buildings in Europe and North America, respectively, to be sheathed in titanium panels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p454_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p454-72">[72]</a></sup> Titanium sheathing was used in the Frederic C. Hamilton Building in Denver, Colorado.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of titanium's superior strength and light weight relative to other metals (steel, stainless steel, and aluminium), and because of recent advances in metalworking techniques, its use has become more widespread in the manufacture of firearms. Primary uses include pistol frames and revolver cylinders. For the same reasons, it is used in the body of laptop computers (for example, in <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple</a>'s PowerBook line).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some upmarket lightweight and corrosion-resistant tools, such as shovels and flashlights, are made of titanium or titanium alloys.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jewelry">Jewelry</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anodized_titanium_table.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3240" data-file-width="4320" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Anodized_titanium_table.jpg/200px-Anodized_titanium_table.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Anodized_titanium_table.jpg/300px-Anodized_titanium_table.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Anodized_titanium_table.jpg/400px-Anodized_titanium_table.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anodized_titanium_table.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Relation between voltage and color for anodized titanium. (Cateb, 2010).</div></div></div>
<p>Because of its durability, titanium has become more popular for designer jewelry (particularly, <a href="/wiki/Titanium_ring" title="Titanium ring">titanium rings</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donachie2000_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Donachie2000-85">[85]</a></sup> Its inertness makes it a good choice for those with allergies or those who will be wearing the jewelry in environments such as swimming pools. Titanium is also <a href="/wiki/Titanium_gold" title="Titanium gold">alloyed with gold</a> to produce an alloy that can be marketed as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carat_(purity)" title="Carat (purity)">24-carat</a> gold because the 1% of alloyed Ti is insufficient to require a lesser mark. The resulting alloy is roughly the hardness of 14-carat gold and is more durable than pure 24-carat gold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>Titanium's durability, light weight, and dent and corrosion resistance make it useful for <a href="/wiki/Watch" title="Watch">watch</a> cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donachie2000_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-Donachie2000-85">[85]</a></sup> Some artists work with titanium to produce sculptures, decorative objects and furniture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>Titanium may be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anodising" title="Anodising">anodized</a> to vary the thickness of the surface oxide layer, causing optical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interference_fringe" title="Interference fringe">interference fringes</a> and a variety of bright colors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> With this coloration and chemical inertness, titanium is a popular metal for <a href="/wiki/Body_piercing" title="Body piercing">body piercing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>Titanium has a minor use in dedicated non-circulating coins and medals. In 1999, Gibraltar released world's first titanium coin for the millennium celebration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Gold_Coast_Titans" title="Gold Coast Titans">Gold Coast Titans</a>, an Australian rugby league team, award a medal of pure titanium to their player of the year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical">Medical</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Titanium_biocompatibility" title="Titanium biocompatibility">Titanium biocompatibility</a></div>
<p>Because titanium is <a href="/wiki/Biocompatibility" title="Biocompatibility">biocompatible</a> (non-toxic and not rejected by the body), it has many medical uses, including surgical implements and implants, such as hip balls and sockets (<a href="/wiki/Joint_replacement" title="Joint replacement">joint replacement</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Dental_implant" title="Dental implant">dental implants</a> that can stay in place for up to 20 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p452_39-5"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p452-39">[39]</a></sup> The titanium is often alloyed with about 4% aluminium or 6% Al and 4% vanadium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titanium_plaatje_voor_pols.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1870" data-file-width="2230" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Titanium_plaatje_voor_pols.jpg/200px-Titanium_plaatje_voor_pols.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Titanium_plaatje_voor_pols.jpg/300px-Titanium_plaatje_voor_pols.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Titanium_plaatje_voor_pols.jpg/400px-Titanium_plaatje_voor_pols.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titanium_plaatje_voor_pols.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Medical screws and plate used for repair fracture of the wrist, scale is in centimeters.</div></div></div>
<p>Titanium has the inherent ability to <a href="/wiki/Osseointegration" title="Osseointegration">osseointegrate</a>, enabling use in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dental_implants" title="Dental implants">dental implants</a> that can last for over 30 years. This property is also useful for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internal_fixator" title="Internal fixator">orthopedic implant</a> applications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p452_39-6"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p452-39">[39]</a></sup> These benefit from titanium's lower modulus of elasticity (<a href="/wiki/Young%27s_modulus" title="Young's modulus">Young's modulus</a>) to more closely match that of the bone that such devices are intended to repair. As a result, skeletal loads are more evenly shared between bone and implant, leading to a lower incidence of bone degradation due to stress shielding and <a href="/wiki/Periprosthetic" title="Periprosthetic">periprosthetic</a> bone fractures, which occur at the boundaries of orthopedic implants. However, titanium alloys' stiffness is still more than twice that of bone, so adjacent bone bears a greatly reduced load and may deteriorate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because titanium is non-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferromagnetic" title="Ferromagnetic">ferromagnetic</a>, patients with titanium implants can be safely examined with <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_resonance_imaging" title="Magnetic resonance imaging">magnetic resonance imaging</a> (convenient for long-term implants). Preparing titanium for implantation in the body involves subjecting it to a high-temperature <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a> arc which removes the surface atoms, exposing fresh titanium that is instantly oxidized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p452_39-7"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p452-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Titanium is used for the <a href="/wiki/Surgical_instrument" title="Surgical instrument">surgical instruments</a> used in <a href="/wiki/Image-guided_surgery" title="Image-guided surgery">image-guided surgery</a>, as well as wheelchairs, crutches, and any other products where high strength and low weight are desirable.
</p><p>Titanium dioxide <a href="/wiki/Nanoparticle" title="Nanoparticle">nanoparticles</a> are widely used in electronics and the delivery of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical_drug" title="Pharmaceutical drug">pharmaceuticals</a> and cosmetics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_waste_storage">Nuclear waste storage</span></h3>
<p>Because of it is corrosion resistance, containers made of titanium have been studied for the long-term storage of nuclear waste. Containers lasting more than 100,000 years are thought possible with manufacturing conditions that minimize material defects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> A titanium "drip shield" could also be installed over containers of other types to enhance their longevity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bioremediation">Bioremediation</span></h2>
<p>The fungal species <i><a href="/wiki/Marasmius_oreades" title="Marasmius oreades">Marasmius oreades</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Hypholoma_capnoides" title="Hypholoma capnoides">Hypholoma capnoides</a></i> can bioconvert titanium in titanium polluted soils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Precautions">Precautions</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kopiva.JPG"><img alt="The dark green dentated elliptic leaves of a nettle" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Kopiva.JPG/220px-Kopiva.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Kopiva.JPG/330px-Kopiva.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Kopiva.JPG/440px-Kopiva.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kopiva.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Urtica_dioica" title="Urtica dioica">Nettles</a> contain up to 80 parts per million of titanium.</div></div></div>
<p>Titanium is non-toxic even in large doses and does not play any natural role inside the <a href="/wiki/Human_body" title="Human body">human body</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p451_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p451-22">[22]</a></sup> An estimated quantity of 0.8 milligrams of titanium is ingested by humans each day, but most passes through without being absorbed in the tissues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p451_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p451-22">[22]</a></sup> It does, however, sometimes <a href="/wiki/Bioaccumulation" title="Bioaccumulation">bio-accumulate</a> in tissues that contain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silica" title="Silica">silica</a>. One study indicates a possible connection between titanium and <a href="/wiki/Yellow_nail_syndrome" title="Yellow nail syndrome">yellow nail syndrome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> An unknown mechanism in <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a> may use titanium to stimulate the production of <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a> and encourage growth. This may explain why most plants contain about 1 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Part_per_million" title="Part per million">part per million</a> (ppm) of titanium, food plants have about 2 ppm, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horsetail" title="Horsetail">horsetail</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urtica" title="Urtica">nettle</a> contain up to 80 ppm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p451_22-4"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p451-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>As a powder or in the form of metal shavings, titanium metal poses a significant fire hazard and, when heated in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air" title="Air">air</a>, an explosion hazard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Water and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> are ineffective for extinguishing a titanium fire; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fire_classes" title="Fire classes">Class D</a> dry powder agents must be used instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HistoryAndUse_7-8"><a href="#cite_note-HistoryAndUse-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>When used in the production or handling of <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a>, titanium should not be exposed to dry chlorine gas because it may result in a titanium–chlorine fire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Even wet chlorine presents a fire hazard when extreme weather conditions cause unexpected drying.
</p><p>Titanium can catch fire when a fresh, non-oxidized surface comes in contact with <a href="/wiki/Liquid_oxygen" title="Liquid oxygen">liquid oxygen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Fresh metal may be exposed when the oxidized surface is struck or scratched with a hard object, or when mechanical strain causes a crack. This poses a limitation to its use in liquid oxygen systems, such as those in the aerospace industry. Because titanium tubing impurities can cause fires when exposed to oxygen, titanium is prohibited in gaseous oxygen respiration systems. Steel tubing is used for high pressure systems (3,000 p.s.i.) and aluminium tubing for low pressure systems.
</p>
